2. **Read remotely** — Use `sg_read_file` to read full file contents from Sourcegraph

**Decision logic:**
1. Know the exact symbol? → `sg_keyword_search`
2. Know the concept, not the name? → `sg_nls_search`
3. Need definition of a symbol? → `sg_go_to_definition`
4. Need all callers/references? → `sg_find_references`
5. Need full file content? → `sg_read_file`

## Scoping (Always Do This)

```
repo:^github.com/ORG/REPO$           # Exact repo (preferred)
repo:github.com/ORG/                 # All repos in org
file:.*\.ts$                         # TypeScript only
file:src/api/                        # Specific directory
```

Start narrow. Expand only if results are empty.

## Efficiency Rules

- Chain searches logically: search → read → references → definition
- Don't re-search for the same pattern; use results from prior calls
- Prefer `sg_keyword_search` over `sg_nls_search` when you have exact terms
- Read 2-3 related files before synthesising, rather than one at a time
- Don't read 20+ remote files without writing code — once you understand the pattern, start implementing

## If Stuck

1. Broaden the search query (synonyms, partial identifiers)
2. Try `sg_nls_search` for semantic matching

4. Use `sg_list_repos` to verify the repository name

# Task: Kubernetes client-go Systems Guide

**Repository:** github.com/sg-evals/kubernetes--8c9c67c0 (mirror of github.com/sg-evals)/kubernetes--8c9c67c0 (mirror of kubernetes/kubernetes) (stripped snapshot)
**Output:** Write your document to `/workspace/documentation.md`

## Objective

Produce an advanced systems guide for client-go that explains API access, controller loops, and extension paths.

## Scope

Focus on the client-go subsystem.
Your document must explain component responsibilities, end-to-end flow, and extension or operational tradeoffs.

## Content Expectations

Address all of the following in your own structure:
- subsystem purpose, boundaries, and upstream/downstream dependencies
- key components and how responsibilities are split
- end-to-end control/request flow with concrete repository evidence
- failure modes, limits, and design tradeoffs
- extension points, customization hooks, and associated risks
- a concise map of the most important files/directories used in your analysis

## Quality Bar

- Cite concrete file paths from the repository.
- Explain behavior and interactions, not just API signatures.
- Include at least one section on operational implications (performance, reliability, or maintainability).
- Do not fabricate classes, functions, or files.

## Anti-Requirements

- Do not output `doc.go`.
- Do not provide shallow bullet dumps without system flow explanation.
- Do not rely on external docs not present in the workspace.

{"content":"1: # `client-go` Architecture\n2: \n3: This document explains the internal architecture of `client-go` for contributors. It describes the\n4: major components, how they interact, and the key design decisions that shape the library.\n5: \n6: ## Client Configuration\n7: \n8: There is an architectural separation between loading client configuration and using it. The\n9: `rest.Config` object is the in-memory representation of this configuration. The\n10: `tools/clientcmd` package is the standard factory for producing it. `clientcmd` handles the\n11: complex logic of parsing `kubeconfig` files, merging contexts, and handling external\n12: authentication providers (e.g., OIDC).\n13: \n14: ## REST Client\n15: \n16: The `rest.Client` is the foundational HTTP client that underpins all other clients. It separates\n17: the low-level concerns of HTTP transport, serialization, and error handling from higher-level,\n18: Kubernetes-specific object logic.\n19: \n20: The `rest.Config` object is used to build the underlying HTTP transport, which is typically a\n21: chain of `http.RoundTripper` objects. Each element in the chain is responsible for a specific\n22: task, such as adding an `Authorization` header. This is the mechanism by which all authentication\n23: is injected into requests.\n24: \n25: The client uses a builder pattern for requests (e.g., `.Verb()`, `.Resource()`), deferring\n26: response processing until a method like `.Into(&pod)` is called. This separation is key to\n27: supporting different client models from a common base.\n28: \n29: ### Endpoint Interactions\n30: \n31: *   **Content Negotiation:** The client uses HTTP `Accept` headers to negotiate the wire format\n32:     (JSON or Protobuf). A key performance optimization using this mechanism is the abili

70-assistanttext-
Perfect! I've created a comprehensive systems guide for client-go at `/workspace/documentation.md`. Here's what the document covers:

## Document Structure

**1. Subsystem Purpose and Boundaries** — Outlines client-go's role as the bridge between user code and the Kubernetes API server, with clear upstream/downstream dependencies.

**2. Key Components and Responsibility Split** — Details 9 major components:
- Transport/Config layer (HTTP client setup, TLS, auth)
- Content negotiation and serialization
- Discovery clients (API introspection)
- Typed clients (code-generated, compile-time safe)
- Dynamic client (generic, CRD-compatible)
- **Informer caching infrastructure** — the most complex subsystem with Reflector, DeltaFIFO, Indexer, SharedInformer, and Listers
- Controller machinery (tying informers to business logic)
- Work queue (decoupling event detection from reconciliation)
- Informer factory (lifecycle management)

**3. End-to-End Flow** — Traces a complete example from Kubernetes API server through Reflector → DeltaFIFO → Indexer → handler notification → work queue → controller reconciliation, with concrete file path evidence.

**4. Failure Modes and Tradeoffs** — Covers 5 common failure modes (resource version too old, handler panics, queue starvation, cache inconsistency, connectivity loss) with root causes and mitigations. Includes performance limits and key architectural tradeoffs (typed vs. dynamic, caching vs. consistency, etc.).

**5. Extension Points** — Explains 15+ customization hooks including custom round-trippers, auth providers, indexes, transformers, rate limiters, and more, with use cases and risks.

**6. Operational Implications** — Practical guidance on performance optimization (scope reduction, selectors, resync tuning, batching), reliability
... (truncated)

  "content": "{\"content\":\"1: # `client-go` Architecture\\n2: \\n3: This document explains the internal architecture of `client-go` for contributors. It describes the\\n4: major components, how they interact, and the key design decisions that shape the library.\\n5: \\n6: ## Client Configuration\\n7: \\n8: There is an architectural separation between loading client configuration and using it. The\\n9: `rest.Config` object is the in-memory representation of this configuration. The\\n10: `tools/clientcmd` package is the standard factory for producing it. `clientcmd` handles the\\n11: complex logic of parsing `kubeconfig` files, merging contexts, and handling external\\n12: authentication providers (e.g., OIDC).\\n13: \\n14: ## REST Client\\n15: \\n16: The `rest.Client` is the foundational HTTP client that underpins all other clients. It separates\\n17: the low-level concerns of HTTP transport, serialization, and error handling from higher-level,\\n18: Kubernetes-specific object logic.\\n19: \\n20: The `rest.Config` object is used to build the underlying HTTP transport, which is typically a\\n21: chain of `http.RoundTripper` objects. Each element in the chain is responsible for a specific\\n22: task, such as adding an `Authorization` header. This is the mechanism by which all authentication\\n23: is injected into requests.\\n24: \\n25: The client uses a builder pattern for requests (e.g., `.Verb()`, `.Resource()`), deferring\\n26: response processing until a method like `.Into(&pod)` is called. This separation is key to\\n27: supporting different client models from a common base.\\n28: \\n29: ### Endpoint Interactions\\n30: \\n31: *   **Content Negotiation:** The client uses HTTP `Accept` headers to negotiate the wire format\\n32:     (JSON or Protobuf). A key performance optimization using this mechanism is the ability to\\n33:     request metadata-only objects via the `as=PartialObjectMetadata;g=meta.k8s.io;v=v1` Accept custom parameter.\\n34:     Also the `as=Table;g=meta.k8s.io;v=v1` Accept custom parameters may be used to request lists as tables. \\n35: *   **Subresources:** The client can target standard subresources like `/status` or `/scale` for\\n36:     object mutations, and it can also handle action-oriented subresources like `/logs` or\\n37:     `/exec`, which often involve streaming data.\\n38: *   **List Pagination:** For `LIST` requests, the client can specify a `limit`. The server will\\n39:     return up to that many items and, if more exist, a `continue` token. The client is\\n40:     responsible for passing this token in a subsequent request to retrieve the next page.\\n41:     Higher-level tools like the `Reflector`'s `ListerWatcher` handle this logic automatically.\\n42: *   **Streaming Watches:** A `WATCH` request returns a `watch.Interface` (from\\n43:     `k8s.io/apimachinery/pkg/watch`), which provides a channel of structured `watch.Event`\\n44:     objects (`ADDED`, `MODIFIED`, `DELETED`, `BOOKMARK`). This decouples the watch consumer from\\n45:     the underlying streaming protocol.\\n46: \\n47: ### Errors, Warnings, and Rate Limiting\\n48: \\n49: *   **Structured Errors:** The client deserializes non-2xx responses into a structured\\n50:     `errors.StatusError`, enabling programmatic error handling (e.g., `errors.IsNotFound(err)`).\\n51: *   **Warnings:** It processes non-fatal `Warning` headers from the API server via a\\n52:     `WarningHandler`.\\n53: *   **Client-Side Rate Limiting:** The `QPS` and `Burst` settings in `rest.Config` are the\\n54:     client's half of the contract with the server's API Priority and Fairness system.\\n55: *   **Server-Side Throttling:** The client's default transport automatically handles HTTP `429`\\n56:     responses by reading the `Retry-After` header, waiting, and retrying the request.\\n57: \\n58: ## Typed and Dynamic Clients\\n59: \\n60: To handle the extensible nature of the Kubernetes API, `client-go` provides two primary client\\n61: models.\\n62: \\n63: The **`kubernetes.Clientset`** provides compile-time, type-safe access to core, built-in APIs.\\n64: \\n65: The **`dynamic.DynamicClient`** represents all objects as `unstructured.Unstructured`, allowing it\\n66: to interact with any API resource, including CRDs. It relies on two discovery mechanisms:\\n67: 1.  The **`discovery.DiscoveryClient`** determines *what* resources exist. The\\n68:     **`CachedDiscoveryClient`** is an optimization that caches this data on disk to solve.\\n69: 2.  The **OpenAPI schema** (fetched from `/openapi/v3`) describes the *structure* of those\\n70:     resources, providing the schema awareness needed by the dynamic client.\\n71: \\n72: ## Code Generation\\n73: \\n74: A core architectural principle of `client-go` is the use of code generation to provide a\\n75: strongly-typed, compile-time-safe interface for specific API GroupVersions. This makes\\n76: controller code more robust and easier to maintain. The tools in `k8s.io/code-generator` produce\\n77: several key components:\\n78: \\n79: *   **Typed Clientsets:** The primary interface for interacting with a specific GroupVersion.\\n80: *   **Typed Listers:** The read-only, cached accessors used by controllers.\\n81: *   **Typed Informers:** The machinery for populating the cache for a specific type.\\n82: *   **Apply Configurations:** The type-safe builders for Server-Side Apply.\\n83: \\n84: A contributor modifying a built-in API type **must** run the code generation scripts to update all\\n85: of these dependent components. For the Kubernetes project, `hack/update-codegen.sh` runs code generation.\\n86: \\n87: `sample-controller` shows how code generate can be configured to build custom controllers.\\n88: \\n89: ## Controller Infrastructure\\n90: \\n91: The `tools/cache` package provides the core infrastructure for controllers, replacing a high-load,\\n92: request-based pattern with a low-load, event-driven, cached model.\\n93: \\n94: The data flow is as follows:\\n95: \\n96: ```mermaid\\n97: graph TD\\n98:     subgraph \\\"Kubernetes API\\\"\\n99:         API_Server[API Server]\\n100:     end\\n101: \\n102:     subgraph \\\"client-go: Informer Mechanism\\\"\\n103:         Reflector(\\\"1. Reflector\\\")\\n104:         DeltaFIFO(\\\"2. DeltaFIFO\\\")\\n105:         Indexer[\\\"3. Indexer (Cache)\\\"]\\n106:         EventHandlers(\\\"4. Event Handlers\\\")\\n107:     end\\n108: \\n109:     subgraph \\\"User Code\\\"\\n110:         WorkQueue[\\\"5. Work Queue\\\"]\\n111:         Controller(\\\"6. Controller\\\")\\n112:     end\\n113: \\n114:     API_Server -- LIST/WATCH --> Reflector\\n115:     Reflector -- Puts changes into --> DeltaFIFO\\n116:     DeltaFIFO -- Is popped by internal loop, which updates --> Indexer\\n117:     Indexer -- Update triggers --> EventHandlers\\n118:     EventHandlers -- Adds key to --> WorkQueue\\n119:     WorkQueue -- Is processed by --> Controller\\n120:     Controller -- Reads from cache via Lister --> Indexer\\n121: ```\\n122: \\n123: A **`Reflector`** performs a `LIST` to get a consistent snapshot of a resource, identified by a\\n124: `resourceVersion`. It then starts a `WATCH` from that `resourceVersion` to receive a continuous\\n125: stream of subsequent changes. The `Reflector`'s relist/rewatch loop is designed to solve the\\n126: **\\\"too old\\\" `resourceVersion` error** by re-listing. To make this recovery more efficient, the\\n127: `Reflector` consumes **watch bookmarks** from the server, which provide a more recent\\n128: `resourceVersion` to restart from.\\n129: \\n130: The **`Lister`** is the primary, read-only, thread-safe interface for a controller's business\\n131: logic to access the `Indexer`'s cache.\\n132: \\n133: ## Controller Patterns\\n134: \\n135: The controller infrastructure is architecturally decoupled from the controller's business logic to\\n136: ensure resiliency.\\n137: \\n138: The **`util/workqueue`** creates a critical boundary between event detection (the informer's job)\\n139: and reconciliation (the controller's job). Informer event handlers only add an object's key to the\\n140: work queue. This allows the controller to retry failed operations with exponential backoff without\\n141: blocking the informer's watch stream.\\n142: \\n143: For high availability, the **`tools/leaderelection`** package provides the standard architectural\\n144: solution to ensure single-writer semantics by having replicas compete to acquire a lock on a\\n145: shared `Lease` object.\\n146: \\n147: ## Server-Side Apply\\n148: \\n149: `client-go` provides a distinct architectural pattern for object mutation that aligns with the\\n150: server's declarative model. This is a separate workflow from the traditional `get-modify-update`\\n151: model that allows multiple controllers to safely co-manage the same object. The\\n152: **`applyconfigurations`** package provides the generated, type-safe builder API used to\\n153: construct the declarative patch.\\n154: \\n155: ## Versioning and Compatibility\\n156: \\n157: `client-go` has a strict versioning relationship with the main Kubernetes repository. A `client-go`\\n158: version `v0.X.Y` corresponds to the Kubernetes version `v1.X.Y`.\\n159: \\n160: The Kubernetes API has strong backward compatibility guarantees: a client built with an older\\n161: version of `client-go` will work with a newer API server. However, the reverse is not guaranteed.\\n162: A contributor must not break compatibility with supported versions of the Kubernetes API server.\\n163: \",\"url\":\"https://sourcegraph.sourcegraph.com/github.com/sg-evals/kubernetes--8c9c67c0@8c9c67c000104450cfc5a5f48053a9a84b73cf93/-/blob/staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/ARCHITECTURE.md\"}",

Each element in the chain is responsible for a specific\n22: task, such as adding an `Authorization` header. This is the mechanism by which all authentication\n23: is injected into requests.\n24: \n25: The client uses a builder pattern for requests (e.g., `.Verb()`, `.Resource()`), deferring\n26: response processing until a method like `.Into(&pod)` is called. This separation is key to\n27: supporting different client models from a common base.\n28: \n29: ### Endpoint Interactions\n30: \n31: *   **Content Negotiation:** The client uses HTTP `Accept` headers to negotiate the wire format\n32:     (JSON or Protobuf). A key performance optimization using this mechanism is the ability to\n33:     request metadata-only objects via the `as=PartialObjectMetadata;g=meta.k8s.io;v=v1` Accept custom parameter.\n34:     Also the `as=Table;g=meta.k8s.io;v=v1` Accept custom parameters may be used to request lists as tables. \n35: *   **Subresources:** The client can target standard subresources like `/status` or `/scale` for\n36:     object mutations, and it can also handle action-oriented subresources like `/logs` or\n37:     `/exec`, which often involve streaming data.\n38: *   **List Pagination:** For `LIST` requests, the client can specify a `limit`. The server will\n39:     return up to that many items and, if more exist, a `continue` token. The client is\n40:     responsible for passing this token in a subsequent request to retrieve the next page.\n41:     Higher-level tools like the `Reflector`'s `ListerWatcher` handle this logic automatically.\n42: *   **Streaming Watches:** A `WATCH` request returns a `watch.Interface` (from\n43:     `k8s.io/apimachinery/pkg/watch`), which provides a channel of structured `watch.Event`\n44:     objects (`ADDED`, `MODIFIED`, `DELETED`, `BOOKMARK`). This decouples the watch consumer from\n45:     the underlying streaming protocol.\n46: \n47: ### Errors, Warnings, and Rate Limiting\n48: \n49: *   **Structured Errors:** The client deserializes non-2xx responses into a structured\n50:     `errors.StatusError`, enabling programmatic error handling (e.g., `errors.IsNotFound(err)`).\n51: *   **Warnings:** It processes non-fatal `Warning` headers from the API server via a\n52:     `WarningHandler`.\n53: *   **Client-Side Rate Limiting:** The `QPS` and `Burst` settings in `rest.Config` are the\n54:     client's half of the contract with the server's API Priority and Fairness system.\n55: *   **Server-Side Throttling:** The client's default transport automatically handles HTTP `429`\n56:     responses by reading the `Retry-After` header, waiting, and retrying the request.\n57: \n58: ## Typed and Dynamic Clients\n59: \n60: To handle the extensible nature of the Kubernetes API, `client-go` provides two primary client\n61: models.\n62: \n63: The **`kubernetes.Clientset`** provides compile-time, type-safe access to core, built-in APIs.\n64: \n65: The **`dynamic.DynamicClient`** represents all objects as `unstructured.Unstructured`, allowing it\n66: to interact with any API resource, including CRDs. It relies on two discovery mechanisms:\n67: 1.  The **`discovery.DiscoveryClient`** determines *what* resources exist. The\n68:     **`CachedDiscoveryClient`** is an optimization that caches this data on disk to solve.\n69: 2.  The **OpenAPI schema** (fetched from `/openapi/v3`) describes the *structure* of those\n70:     resources, providing the schema awareness needed by the dynamic client.\n71: \n72: ## Code Generation\n73: \n74: A core architectural principle of `client-go` is the use of code generation to provide a\n75: strongly-typed, compile-time-safe interface for specific API GroupVersions. This makes\n76: controller code more robust and easier to maintain. The tools in `k8s.io/code-generator` produce\n77: several key components:\n78: \n79: *   **Typed Clientsets:** The primary interface for interacting with a specific GroupVersion.\n80: *   **Typed Listers:** The read-only, cached accessors used by controllers.\n81: *   **Typed Informers:** The machinery for populating the cache for a specific type.\n82: *   **Apply Configurations:** The type-safe builders for Server-Side Apply.\n83: \n84: A contributor modifying a built-in API type **must** run the code generation scripts to update all\n85: of these dependent components. For the Kubernetes project, `hack/update-codegen.sh` runs code generation.\n86: \n87: `sample-controller` shows how code generate can be configured to build custom controllers.\n88: \n89: ## Controller Infrastructure\n90: \n91: The `tools/cache` package provides the core infrastructure for controllers, replacing a high-load,\n92: request-based pattern with a low-load, event-driven, cached model.\n93: \n94: The data flow is as follows:\n95: \n96: ```mermaid\n97: graph TD\n98:     subgraph \"Kubernetes API\"\n99:         API_Server[API Server]\n100:     end\n101: \n102:     subgraph \"client-go: Informer Mechanism\"\n103:         Reflector(\"1. Reflector\")\n104:         DeltaFIFO(\"2. DeltaFIFO\")\n105:         Indexer[\"3. Indexer (Cache)\"]\n106:         EventHandlers(\"4. Event Handlers\")\n107:     end\n108: \n109:     subgraph \"User Code\"\n110:         WorkQueue[\"5. Work Queue\"]\n111:         Controller(\"6. Controller\")\n112:     end\n113: \n114:     API_Server -- LIST/WATCH --> Reflector\n115:     Reflector -- Puts changes into --> DeltaFIFO\n116:     DeltaFIFO -- Is popped by internal loop, which updates --> Indexer\n117:     Indexer -- Update triggers --> EventHandlers\n118:     EventHandlers -- Adds key to --> WorkQueue\n119:     WorkQueue -- Is processed by --> Controller\n120:     Controller -- Reads from cache via Lister --> Indexer\n121: ```\n122: \n123: A **`Reflector`** performs a `LIST` to get a consistent snapshot of a resource, identified by a\n124: `resourceVersion`. It then starts a `WATCH` from that `resourceVersion` to receive a continuous\n125: stream of subsequent changes. The `Reflector`'s relist/rewatch loop is designed to solve the\n126: **\"too old\" `resourceVersion` error** by re-listing. To make this recovery more efficient, the\n127: `Reflector` consumes **watch bookmarks** from the server, which provide a more recent\n128: `resourceVersion` to restart from.\n129: \n130: The **`Lister`** is the primary, read-only, thread-safe interface for a controller's business\n131: logic to access the `Indexer`'s cache.\n132: \n133: ## Controller Patterns\n134: \n135: The controller infrastructure is architecturally decoupled from the controller's business logic to\n136: ensure resiliency.\n137: \n138: The **`util/workqueue`** creates a critical boundary between event detection (the informer's job)\n139: and reconciliation (the controller's job). Informer event handlers only add an object's key to the\n140: work queue. This allows the controller to retry failed operations with exponential backoff without\n141: blocking the informer's watch stream.\n142: \n143: For high availability, the **`tools/leaderelection`** package provides the standard architectural\n144: solution to ensure single-writer semantics by having replicas compete to acquire a lock on a\n145: shared `Lease` object.\n146: \n147: ## Server-Side Apply\n148: \n149: `client-go` provides a distinct architectural pattern for object mutation that aligns with the\n150: server's declarative model. This is a separate workflow from the traditional `get-modify-update`\n151: model that allows multiple controllers to safely co-manage the same object. The\n152: **`applyconfigurations`** package provides the generated, type-safe builder API used to\n153: construct the declarative patch.\n154: \n155: ## Versioning and Compatibility\n156: \n157: `client-go` has a strict versioning relationship with the main Kubernetes repository. A `client-go`\n158: version `v0.X.Y` corresponds to the Kubernetes version `v1.X.Y`.\n159: \n160: The Kubernetes API has strong backward compatibility guarantees: a client built with an older\n161: version of `client-go` will work with a newer API server. However, the reverse is not guaranteed.\n162: A contributor must not break compatibility with supported versions of the Kubernetes API server.\n163: ",
    "url": "https://sourcegraph.sourcegraph.com/github.com/sg-evals/kubernetes--8c9c67c0@8c9c67c000104450cfc5a5f48053a9a84b73cf93/-/blob/staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/ARCHITECTURE.md"
  }
}

All are\n32: // optional.\n33: type DeltaFIFOOptions struct {\n34: \n35: \t// KeyFunction is used to figure out what key an object should have. (It's\n36: \t// exposed in the returned DeltaFIFO's KeyOf() method, with additional\n37: \t// handling around deleted objects and queue state).\n38: \t// Optional, the default is MetaNamespaceKeyFunc.\n39: \tKeyFunction KeyFunc\n40: \n41: \t// KnownObjects is expected to return a list of keys that the consumer of\n42: \t// this queue \"knows about\". It is used to decide which items are missing\n43: \t// when Replace() is called; 'Deleted' deltas are produced for the missing items.\n44: \t// KnownObjects may be nil if you can tolerate missing deletions on Replace().\n45: \tKnownObjects KeyListerGetter\n46: \n47: \t// EmitDeltaTypeReplaced indicates that the queue consumer\n48: \t// understands the Replaced DeltaType. Before the `Replaced` event type was\n49: \t// added, calls to Replace() were handled the same as Sync(). For\n50: \t// backwards-compatibility purposes, this is false by default.\n51: \t// When true, `Replaced` events will be sent for items passed to a Replace() call.\n52: \t// When false, `Sync` events will be sent instead.\n53: \tEmitDeltaTypeReplaced bool\n54: \n55: \t// If set, will be called for objects before enqueueing them. Please\n56: \t// see the comment on TransformFunc for details.\n57: \tTransformer TransformFunc\n58: \n59: \t// If set, log output will go to this logger instead of klog.Background().\n60: \tLogger *klog.Logger\n61: }\n62: \n63: // DeltaFIFO is like FIFO, but differs in two ways.  One is that the\n64: // accumulator associated with a given object's key is not that object\n65: // but rather a Deltas, which is a slice of Delta values for that\n66: // object.  Applying an object to a Deltas means to append a Delta\n67: // except when the potentially appended Delta is a Deleted and the\n68: // Deltas already ends with a Deleted.  In that case the Deltas does\n69: // not grow, although the terminal Deleted will be replaced by the new\n70: // Deleted if the older Deleted's object is a\n71: // DeletedFinalStateUnknown.\n72: //\n73: // The other difference is that DeltaFIFO has two additional ways that\n74: // an object can be applied to an accumulator: Replaced and Sync.\n75: // If EmitDeltaTypeReplaced is not set to true, Sync will be used in\n76: // replace events for backwards compatibility.  Sync is used for periodic\n77: // resync events.\n78: //\n79: // DeltaFIFO is a producer-consumer queue, where a Reflector is\n80: // intended to be the producer, and the consumer is whatever calls\n81: // the Pop() method.\n82: //\n83: // DeltaFIFO solves this use case:\n84: //   - You want to process every object change (delta) at most once.\n85: //   - When you process an object, you want to see everything\n86: //     that's happened to it since you last processed it.\n87: //   - You want to process the deletion of some of the objects.\n88: //   - You might want to periodically reprocess objects.\n89: //\n90: // DeltaFIFO's Pop(), Get(), and GetByKey() methods return\n91: // interface{} to satisfy the Store/Queue interfaces, but they\n92: // will always return an object of type Deltas. List() returns\n93: // the newest object from each accumulator in the FIFO.\n94: //\n95: // A DeltaFIFO's knownObjects KeyListerGetter provides the abilities\n96: // to list Store keys and to get objects by Store key.  The objects in\n97: // question are called \"known objects\" and this set of objects\n98: // modifies the behavior of the Delete, Replace, and Resync methods\n99: // (each in a different way).\n100: //\n101: // A note on threading: If you call Pop() in parallel from multiple\n102: // threads, you could end up with multiple threads processing slightly\n103: // different versions of the same object.\n104: type DeltaFIFO struct {\n105: \t// lock/cond protects access to 'items' and 'queue'.\n106: \tlock sync.RWMutex\n107: \tcond sync.Cond\n108: \n109: \t// `items` maps a key to a Deltas.\n110: \t// Each such Deltas has at least one Delta.\n111: \titems map[string]Deltas\n112: \n113: \t// `queue` maintains FIFO order of keys for consumption in Pop().\n114: \t// There are no duplicates in `queue`.\n115: \t// A key is in `queue` if and only if it is in `items`.\n116: \tqueue []string\n117: \n118: \t// populated is true if the first batch of items inserted by Replace() has been populated\n119: \t// or Delete/Add/Update/AddIfNotPresent was called first.\n120: \tpopulated bool\n121: \t// initialPopulationCount is the number of items inserted by the first call of Replace()\n122: \tinitialPopulationCount int\n123: \n124: \t// keyFunc is used to make the key used for queued item\n125: \t// insertion and retrieval, and should be deterministic.\n126: \tkeyFunc KeyFunc\n127: \n128: \t// knownObjects list keys that are \"known\" --- affecting Delete(),\n129: \t// Replace(), and Resync()\n130: \tknownObjects KeyListerGetter\n131: \n132: \t// Used to indicate a queue is closed so a control loop can exit when a queue is empty.\n133: \t// Currently, not used to gate any of CRUD operations.\n134: \tclosed bool\n135: \n136: \t// emitDeltaTypeReplaced is whether to emit the Replaced or Sync\n137: \t// DeltaType when Replace() is called (to preserve backwards compat).\n138: \temitDeltaTypeReplaced bool\n139: \n140: \t// Called with every object if non-nil.\n141: \ttransformer TransformFunc\n142: \n143: \t// logger is a per-instance logger. This gets chosen when constructing\n144: \t// the instance, with klog.Background() as default.\n145: \tlogger klog.Logger\n146: }\n147: \n148: // TransformFunc allows for transforming an object before it will be processed.\n149: //\n150: // The most common usage pattern is to clean-up some parts of the object to\n151: ",

An object is identified by its API group, kind/resource,\\n44: // namespace (if any), and name; the `ObjectMeta.UID` is not part of\\n45: // an object's ID as far as this contract is concerned.  One\\n46: // SharedInformer provides linkage to objects of a particular API\\n47: // group and kind/resource.  The linked object collection of a\\n48: // SharedInformer may be further restricted to one namespace (if\\n49: // applicable) and/or by label selector and/or field selector.\\n50: //\\n51: // The authoritative state of an object is what apiservers provide\\n52: // access to, and an object goes through a strict sequence of states.\\n53: // An object state is either (1) present with a ResourceVersion and\\n54: // other appropriate content or (2) \\\"absent\\\".\\n55: //\\n56: // A SharedInformer maintains a local cache --- exposed by GetStore(),\\n57: // by GetIndexer() in the case of an indexed informer, and possibly by\\n58: // machinery involved in creating and/or accessing the informer --- of\\n59: // the state of each relevant object.  This cache is eventually\\n60: // consistent with the authoritative state.  This means that, unless\\n61: // prevented by persistent communication problems, if ever a\\n62: // particular object ID X is authoritatively associated with a state S\\n63: // then for every SharedInformer I whose collection includes (X, S)\\n64: // eventually either (1) I's cache associates X with S or a later\\n65: // state of X, (2) I is stopped, or (3) the authoritative state\\n66: // service for X terminates.  To be formally complete, we say that the\\n67: // absent state meets any restriction by label selector or field\\n68: // selector.\\n69: //\\n70: // For a given informer and relevant object ID X, the sequence of\\n71: // states that appears in the informer's cache is a subsequence of the\\n72: // states authoritatively associated with X.  That is, some states\\n73: // might never appear in the cache but ordering among the appearing\\n74: // states is correct.  Note, however, that there is no promise about\\n75: // ordering between states seen for different objects.\\n76: //\\n77: // The local cache starts out empty, and gets populated and updated\\n78: // during `Run()`.\\n79: //\\n80: // As a simple example, if a collection of objects is henceforth\\n81: // unchanging, a SharedInformer is created that links to that\\n82: // collection, and that SharedInformer is `Run()` then that\\n83: // SharedInformer's cache eventually holds an exact copy of that\\n84: // collection (unless it is stopped too soon, the authoritative state\\n85: // service ends, or communication problems between the two\\n86: // persistently thwart achievement).\\n87: //\\n88: // As another simple example, if the local cache ever holds a\\n89: // non-absent state for some object ID and the object is eventually\\n90: // removed from the authoritative state then eventually the object is\\n91: // removed from the local cache (unless the SharedInformer is stopped\\n92: // too soon, the authoritative state service ends, or communication\\n93: // problems persistently thwart the desired result).\\n94: //\\n95: // The keys in the Store are of the form namespace/name for namespaced\\n96: // objects, and are simply the name for non-namespaced objects.\\n97: // Clients can use `MetaNamespaceKeyFunc(obj)` to extract the key for\\n98: // a given object, and `SplitMetaNamespaceKey(key)` to split a key\\n99: // into its constituent parts.\\n100: //\\n101: // Every query against the local cache is answered entirely from one\\n102: // snapshot of the cache's state.  Thus, the result of a `List` call\\n103: // will not contain two entries with the same namespace and name.\\n104: //\\n105: // A client is identified here by a ResourceEventHandler.  For every\\n106: // update to the SharedInformer's local cache and for every client\\n107: // added before `Run()`, eventually either the SharedInformer is\\n108: // stopped or the client is notified of the update.  A client added\\n109: // after `Run()` starts gets a startup batch of notifications of\\n110: // additions of the objects existing in the cache at the time that\\n111: // client was added; also, for every update to the SharedInformer's\\n112: // local cache after that client was added, eventually either the\\n113: // SharedInformer is stopped or that client is notified of that\\n114: // update.  Client notifications happen after the corresponding cache\\n115: // update and, in the case of a SharedIndexInformer, after the\\n116: // corresponding index updates.  It is possible that additional cache\\n117: // and index updates happen before such a prescribed notification.\\n118: // For a given SharedInformer and client, the notifications are\\n119: // delivered sequentially.  For a given SharedInformer, client, and\\n120: // object ID, the notifications are delivered in order.  Because\\n121: // `ObjectMeta.UID` has no role in identifying objects, it is possible\\n122: // that when (1) object O1 with ID (e.g. namespace and name) X and\\n123: // `ObjectMeta.UID` U1 in the SharedInformer's local cache is deleted\\n124: // and later (2) another object O2 with ID X and ObjectMeta.UID U2 is\\n125: // created the informer's clients are not notified of (1) and (2) but\\n126: // rather are notified only of an update from O1 to O2. Clients that\\n127: // need to detect such cases might do so by comparing the `ObjectMeta.UID`\\n128: // field of the old and the new object in the code that handles update\\n129: // notifications (i.e. `OnUpdate` method of ResourceEventHandler).\\n130: //\\n131: // A client must process each notification promptly; a SharedInformer\\n132: // is not engineered to deal well with a large backlog of\\n133: // notifications to deliver.  Lengthy processing should be passed off\\n134: // to something else, for example through a\\n135: // `client-go/util/workqueue`.\\n136: //\\n137: // A delete notification exposes the last locally known non-absent\\n138: // state, except that its ResourceVersion is replaced with a\\n139: // ResourceVersion in which the object is actually absent.\\n140: type SharedInformer interface {\\n141: \\t// AddEventHandler adds an event handler to the shared informer using\\n142: \\t// the shared informer's resync period. In case an error happened, it has to simply return the error.\n75: // The retry logic should not be part of the business logic.\n76: func (c *Controller) syncToStdout(key string) error {\n77: \tobj, exists, err := c.indexer.GetByKey(key)\n78: \tif err != nil {\n79: \t\tklog.Errorf(\"Fetching object with key %s from store failed with %v\", key, err)\n80: \t\treturn err\n81: \t}\n82: \n83: \tif !exists {\n84: \t\t// Below we will warm up our cache with a Pod, so that we will see a delete for one pod\n85: \t\tfmt.Printf(\"Pod %s does not exist anymore\\n\", key)\n86: \t} else {\n87: \t\t// Note that you also have to check the uid if you have a local controlled resource, which\n88: \t\t// is dependent on the actual instance, to detect that a Pod was recreated with the same name\n89: \t\tfmt.Printf(\"Sync/Add/Update for Pod %s\\n\", obj.(*v1.Pod).GetName())\n90: \t}\n91: \treturn nil\n92: }\n93: \n94: // handleErr checks if an error happened and makes sure we will retry later.\n95: func (c *Controller) handleErr(err error, key string) {\n96: \tif err == nil {\n97: \t\t// Forget about the #AddRateLimited history of the key on every successful synchronization.\n98: \t\t// This ensures that future processing of updates for this key is not delayed because of\n99: \t\t// an outdated error history.\n100: \t\tc.queue.Forget(key)\n101: \t\treturn\n102: \t}\n103: \n104: \t// This controller retries 5 times if something goes wrong. After that, it stops trying.\n105: \tif c.queue.NumRequeues(key) < 5 {\n106: \t\tklog.Infof(\"Error syncing pod %v: %v\", key, err)\n107: \n108: \t\t// Re-enqueue the key rate limited. Based on the rate limiter on the\n109: \t\t// queue and the re-enqueue history, the key will be processed later again.\n110: \t\tc.queue.AddRateLimited(key)\n111: \t\treturn\n112: \t}\n113: \n114: \tc.queue.Forget(key)\n115: \t// Report to an external entity that, even after several retries, we could not successfully process this key\n116: \truntime.HandleError(err)\n117: \tklog.Infof(\"Dropping pod %q out of the queue: %v\", key, err)\n118: }\n119: \n120: // Run begins watching and syncing.\n121: func (c *Controller) Run(ctx context.Context, workers int) {\n122: \tdefer runtime.HandleCrashWithContext(ctx)\n123: \n124: \t// Let the workers stop when we are done\n125: \tdefer c.queue.ShutDown()\n126: \tklog.Info(\"Starting Pod controller\")\n127: \n128: \tgo c.informer.RunWithContext(ctx)\n129: \n130: \t// Wait for all involved caches to be synced, before processing items from the queue is started\n131: \tif !cache.WaitForNamedCacheSyncWithContext(ctx, c.informer.HasSynced) {\n132: \t\truntime.HandleError(fmt.Errorf(\"Timed out waiting for caches to sync\"))\n133: \t\treturn\n134: \t}\n135: \n136: \tfor i := 0; i < workers; i++ {\n137: \t\tgo wait.UntilWithContext(ctx, c.runWorker, time.Second)\n138: \t}\n139: \n140: \t<-ctx.Done()\n141: \tklog.Info(\"Stopping Pod controller\")\n142: }\n143: \n144: func (c *Controller) runWorker(ctx context.Context) {\n145: \tfor c.processNextItem() {\n146: \t}\n147: }\n148: \n149: func main() {\n150: \tvar kubeconfig string\n151: \tvar master string\n152: \n153: \tflag.StringVar(&kubeconfig, \"kubeconfig\", \"\", \"absolute path to the kubeconfig file\")\n154: \tflag.StringVar(&master, \"master\", \"\", \"master url\")\n155: \tflag.Parse()\n156: \n157: \t// creates the connection\n158: \tconfig, err := clientcmd.BuildConfigFromFlags(master, kubeconfig)\n159: \tif err != nil {\n160: \t\tklog.Fatal(err)\n161: \t}\n162: \n163: \t// creates the clientset\n164: \tclientset, err := kubernetes.NewForConfig(config)\n165: \tif err != nil {\n166: \t\tklog.Fatal(err)\n167: \t}\n168: \n169: \tctx := context.Background()\n170: \n171: \t// create the pod watcher\n172: \tpodListWatcher := cache.NewListWatchFromClient(clientset.CoreV1().RESTClient(), \"pods\", v1.NamespaceDefault, fields.Everything())\n173: \n174: \t// create the workqueue\n175: \tqueue := workqueue.NewTypedRateLimitingQueue(workqueue.DefaultTypedControllerRateLimiter[string]())\n176: \n177: \t// Bind the workqueue to a cache with the help of an informer. This way we make sure that\n178: \t// whenever the cache is updated, the pod key is added to the workqueue.\n179: \t// Note that when we finally process the item from the workqueue, we might see a newer version\n180: \t// of the Pod than the version which was responsible for triggering the update.\n181: \tindexer, informer := cache.NewIndexerInformer(podListWatcher, &v1.Pod{}, 0, cache.ResourceEventHandlerFuncs{\n182: \t\tAddFunc: func(obj interface{}) {\n183: \t\t\tkey, err := cache.MetaNamespaceKeyFunc(obj)\n184: \t\t\tif err == nil {\n185: \t\t\t\tqueue.Add(key)\n186: \t\t\t}\n187: \t\t},\n188: \t\tUpdateFunc: func(old interface{}, new interface{}) {\n189: \t\t\tkey, err := cache.MetaNamespaceKeyFunc(new)\n190: \t\t\tif err == nil {\n191: \t\t\t\tqueue.Add(key)\n192: \t\t\t}\n193: \t\t},\n194: \t\tDeleteFunc: func(obj interface{}) {\n195: \t\t\t// IndexerInformer uses a delta queue, therefore for deletes we have to use this\n196: \t\t\t// key function.\n197: \t\t\tkey, err := cache.DeletionHandlingMetaNamespaceKeyFunc(obj)\n198: \t\t\tif err == nil {\n199: \t\t\t\tqueue.Add(key)\n200: \t\t\t}\n201: ",
